# 51a History of Friend, Enemy and Power

*A comprehensive exploration of the friend-enemy distinction and power dynamics across political philosophy, from ancient to contemporary thought*

## Introduction

The distinction between friend and enemy, and its relationship to power, represents one of the most enduring themes in political philosophy. While Carl Schmitt's 1927 work *The Concept of the Political* crystallized this distinction as the defining criterion of the political realm, the underlying concepts traverse millennia of philosophical, theological, and political thought. This document provides a comprehensive exploration of these interconnected themes, tracing their development from ancient philosophy through contemporary political theory.

The friend-enemy distinction raises fundamental questions about political identity, sovereignty, legitimacy, and the nature of conflict itself. It challenges liberal assumptions about the possibility of depoliticization and forces us to confront the existential dimensions of political community. As Schmitt argues, the enemy is "the other, the stranger; and it is sufficient for his nature that he is, in a specially intense way, existentially something different and alien, so that in the extreme case conflicts with him are possible."

*(Source: Schmitt, Carl. The Concept of the Political. University of Chicago Press, 2007.)*

---

## Ancient Foundations

### Thucydides and Realpolitik

The foundations of political realism, which emphasizes power and the friend-enemy distinction, can be traced to ancient Greek thought. Thucydides (c. 460-400 BCE), in his *History of the Peloponnesian War*, provides one of the earliest systematic accounts of power politics. The famous **Melian Dialogue** demonstrates the brutal logic of political enmity: "The strong do what they can and the weak suffer what they must." This stark formulation reveals the existential nature of political conflict when divorced from moral considerations.

Thucydides' account illustrates how political communities define themselves through their relationships with other polities—relationships fundamentally structured by calculations of power, security, and survival. The Athenian decision to destroy Melos despite its neutrality demonstrates how the friend-enemy distinction can override moral considerations when a state perceives its interests or existence to be at stake.

*(Source: Thucydides. The History of the Peloponnesian War, trans. Richard Crawley. Project Gutenberg.)*

### Plato: Justice, Power, and the Ideal State

Plato (428-348 BCE) in *The Republic* grapples with the relationship between justice and power. Through the character of Thrasymachus, Plato presents the argument that "justice is nothing other than the advantage of the stronger"—a view he ultimately rejects in favor of a transcendent conception of justice. However, *The Republic* acknowledges the importance of the distinction between friend and enemy for the political community.

Plato's ideal state requires guardians who can distinguish between the friendly and the hostile, protecting the city from external enemies while maintaining internal harmony. The education of the guardians focuses on cultivating thumos (spiritedness) that can be directed appropriately—fierce toward enemies, gentle toward friends and fellow citizens. This recognition of political enmity as a permanent feature of political life coexists with Plato's philosophical idealism.

*(Source: Plato. The Republic, trans. G.M.A. Grube. Hackett Publishing Company, 1992.)*

### Aristotle: Political Community and Friendship

Aristotle (384-322 BCE) provides a more naturalistic account of political community in his *Politics* and explores the nature of friendship extensively in the *Nicomachean Ethics*. For Aristotle, humans are "political animals" (zoon politikon) by nature, and political community exists not merely for survival but for living well (eu zen).

Aristotle distinguishes three types of friendship: friendships of utility, pleasure, and virtue. The highest form—friendship based on virtue—involves mutual recognition and goodwill between equals who wish each other well for their own sake. This concept of civic friendship (philia politike) becomes essential to Aristotle's conception of the well-ordered polis. Political friendship binds citizens together through shared values and common purposes, creating the cohesion necessary for political life.

However, Aristotle also recognizes that political communities exist in a world of scarcity and competition. The *Politics* discusses just and unjust wars, natural slavery, and the proper treatment of conquered peoples—all acknowledging that politics involves relationships of enmity as well as friendship.

*(Sources: Aristotle. Politics, trans. C.D.C. Reeve. Hackett Publishing Company, 1998; Aristotle. Nicomachean Ethics, trans. Terence Irwin. Hackett Publishing Company, 1999.)*

### The Philoi: Ancient Greek Bonds of Loyalty

The ancient Greek concept of *philoi* encompassed friends, family members, and political allies bound by reciprocal obligations and duties. This network of relationships was fundamental to Greek social and political life. The *philoi* relationship was characterized by mutual aid, loyalty, and the expectation of reciprocity—what one might call a politics of friendship that structured much of Greek civic life.

The clear distinction between *philoi* (friends/insiders) and *echthroi* (enemies/outsiders) shaped Greek warfare, diplomacy, and domestic politics. This binary structure anticipated later formulations of the friend-enemy distinction, though embedded in a different cultural and political context.

*(Source: Ancient Greek social structures. See: Herman, Gabriel. Ritualised Friendship and the Greek City. Cambridge University Press, 1987.)*

### Sun Tzu: Strategy, Deception, and the Enemy

In ancient Chinese thought, Sun Tzu's *The Art of War* (5th century BCE) provides a sophisticated analysis of conflict, strategy, and the nature of enmity. Sun Tzu's famous maxim "Know your enemy and know yourself" elevates the understanding of the adversary to a central strategic principle. The work treats enmity as a relationship requiring careful study, cunning, and strategic thinking rather than merely brute force.

Sun Tzu's approach differs from Western political realism in its emphasis on deception, indirect approach, and the subordination of military conflict to political objectives. "The supreme art of war is to subdue the enemy without fighting." This formulation suggests that power manifests not only through violence but through the skillful manipulation of appearances and the enemy's perceptions.

The text also contains the proto-Machiavellian insight that a wise ruler sometimes must treat allies and enemies with calculated pragmatism: "Keep your friends close and your enemies closer." This recognition of the fluidity of political alliances and enmities would resonate through subsequent strategic thought.

*(Source: Sun Tzu. The Art of War, trans. Samuel B. Griffith. Oxford University Press, 1971.)*

### The Mandala Theory: Enemy of My Enemy

Ancient Indian political thought, particularly as articulated in Kautilya's *Arthashastra* (c. 300 BCE), developed the **Mandala** (circle) theory of interstate relations. This theory provides a geometric model of friend-enemy relationships: one's immediate neighbors are natural enemies, the neighbors beyond them are natural friends (as they are enemies of one's enemies), and so on in concentric circles.

This systematic theorization of the principle "the enemy of my enemy is my friend" demonstrates an early recognition of how the friend-enemy distinction structures international relations through a logic of strategic necessity rather than ideological affinity or moral affiliation.

*(Source: Kautilya. The Arthashastra, trans. L.N. Rangarajan. Penguin Books, 1992.)*

---

## Medieval and Early Modern Thought

### St. Augustine: The Two Cities

St. Augustine of Hippo (354-430 CE) in *The City of God* develops a theological framework that profoundly influenced subsequent Christian political thought. Augustine distinguishes between the City of God (civitas Dei) and the earthly city (civitas terrena), characterized by their respective loves: love of God versus love of self.

This distinction creates a theological foundation for political enmity. Christians, as pilgrims in the earthly city, exist in a state of tension with worldly powers and with those who reject divine authority. Augustine's framework suggests that ultimate political peace is impossible in the fallen world—true peace belongs only to the City of God. The earthly city is characterized by libido dominandi (lust for domination), making conflict, domination, and enmity permanent features of terrestrial political life.

Augustine's concept of "just war" acknowledges that Christians may be obligated to wage war against enemies of God and justice, though always with sorrow rather than hatred. This theological sanctioning of violence against enemies under certain conditions would have lasting influence on Western political theology.

*(Source: Augustine of Hippo. The City of God, trans. Henry Bettenson. Penguin Classics, 2003.)*

### Machiavelli: Political Realism and Virtù

Niccolò Machiavelli (1469-1527) represents a decisive break with medieval Christian political thought. In *The Prince* (1532) and *Discourses on Livy*, Machiavelli offers a stark, realistic account of political power stripped of moral and theological pretensions.

Machiavelli's famous advice—"it is better to be feared than loved"—reflects his understanding of power as rooted in the capacity to compel through force or the threat of force. The successful prince must know how to recognize enemies, how to eliminate them when necessary, and how to convert enemies into friends through skillful politics.

Key Machiavellian insights include:

- **The autonomy of politics**: Political action operates according to its own logic (ragione di stato), distinct from private morality
- **The fickleness of political friendship**: Today's ally may be tomorrow's enemy; political relationships are inherently unstable
- **The necessity of force**: "All armed prophets have conquered, and the unarmed ones have been destroyed"
- **Apparent versus real qualities**: The effective prince must appear merciful, faithful, humane, religious, and upright—but be prepared to act contrary to these virtues when necessary

Machiavelli's realism influenced later theorists of power politics, including Schmitt, who saw Machiavelli as recognizing the permanent possibility of political conflict and the necessity of maintaining the capacity for organized violence.

*(Source: Machiavelli, Niccolò. The Prince, trans. Harvey C. Mansfield. University of Chicago Press, 1998.)*

---

## Enlightenment and Modern Foundations

### Thomas Hobbes: State of Nature and Sovereign Power

Thomas Hobbes (1588-1679) provides perhaps the most influential modern foundation for thinking about the friend-enemy distinction in his masterwork *Leviathan* (1651). Hobbes's account of the "state of nature" as a "war of all against all" (bellum omnium contra omnes) presents enmity as the natural condition of humanity in the absence of a sovereign power.

In the state of nature, according to Hobbes, human life is "solitary, poor, nasty, brutish, and short." Each individual has a right to everything, including the body of every other person, making everyone potentially an enemy of everyone else. Fear of violent death drives individuals to seek peace through the establishment of a sovereign authority capable of overawing them all.

Key Hobbesian concepts:

- **The sovereign as decision-maker**: The sovereign decides who is friend and who is enemy, both domestically and internationally
- **Protection and obedience**: The obligation to obey the sovereign extends only as far as the sovereign's ability to provide protection
- **The permanence of international enmity**: While domestic peace is possible under a sovereign, international relations remain in a state of nature—a war of all states against all states
- **Authority, not truth, makes law**: *Auctoritas, non veritas, facit legem*—a principle Schmitt would later emphasize

Hobbes's influence on Schmitt is profound. Schmitt saw Hobbes as the first truly modern political philosopher, one who understood that the political relationship is fundamentally defined by the capacity to determine life and death.

*(Source: Hobbes, Thomas. Leviathan, ed. Richard Tuck. Cambridge University Press, 1996.)*

### Spinoza: Political Theology and the Multitude

Baruch Spinoza (1632-1677) in his *Theological-Political Treatise* and *Political Treatise* develops an alternative account of sovereignty and power. While influenced by Hobbes, Spinoza emphasizes the collective power of the multitude rather than the concentrated power of the sovereign.

For Spinoza, power is not primarily about domination but about capacity—the potentia (power to act) rather than merely potestas (power over others). This shift in emphasis would later influence radical democratic theory and alternative conceptions of constituent power.

Spinoza also provides crucial insights into the relationship between religion and politics, showing how theological concepts can be mobilized to create political enemies and how the distinction between the faithful and the heretic structures political community.

*(Source: Spinoza, Baruch. Theological-Political Treatise, trans. Michael Silverthorne and Jonathan Israel. Cambridge University Press, 2007.)*

### John Locke: Natural Rights and Limited Government

John Locke (1632-1704) offers a liberal alternative to Hobbes's authoritarian solution. In *Two Treatises of Government*, Locke argues that the state of nature, while inconvenient, is not necessarily a state of war. Individuals possess natural rights to life, liberty, and property that exist prior to and independent of political authority.

The Lockean framework creates a distinction between:
- **Just enemies**: Those who violate natural rights and may be punished
- **Political society**: Constituted to better protect natural rights through impartial adjudication

Locke's liberalism seeks to domesticate enmity through law, property, and consent. The friend-enemy distinction is not eliminated but channeled through legal and constitutional procedures. This liberal attempt to depoliticize conflict through juridification would become a central target of Schmitt's critique.

*(Source: Locke, John. Two Treatises of Government, ed. Peter Laslett. Cambridge University Press, 1988.)*

### Jean-Jacques Rousseau: General Will and Popular Sovereignty

Jean-Jacques Rousseau (1712-1778) in *The Social Contract* (1762) develops a theory of popular sovereignty that would prove influential for both democratic and authoritarian political thought. Rousseau's concept of the "general will" (volonté générale) creates a vision of political unity that transcends individual interests.

Key Rousseauian concepts:

- **The general will cannot be represented**: It must express itself directly, pointing toward direct democracy or plebiscitary politics
- **Civic religion**: Rousseau argues for a civil religion that binds citizens together through shared belief
- **The paradox of forcing to be free**: Those who refuse to obey the general will can be "forced to be free"—a formulation that troubled later liberals

Rousseau's emphasis on homogeneity and the need for civic virtue influenced later nationalist and populist movements. His work suggests that genuine political community requires a degree of unity that may necessitate the exclusion or suppression of those who do not share in the common identity.

*(Source: Rousseau, Jean-Jacques. The Social Contract, trans. Maurice Cranston. Penguin Classics, 1968.)*

### Immanuel Kant: Perpetual Peace and Cosmopolitanism

Immanuel Kant (1724-1804) represents the high-water mark of Enlightenment cosmopolitanism. In *Perpetual Peace* (1795), Kant argues that reason demands the gradual abolition of war through a federation of free states, republican constitutions, and cosmopolitan right.

Kant's vision involves:
- **Republican constitutions**: Separating the decision to go to war from those who will bear its costs
- **Federation of free states**: A non-coercive alliance of republics that renounce war against each other
- **Cosmopolitan right**: Universal hospitality and the recognition of common humanity

Kant's project aims to transcend the friend-enemy distinction through the universal application of reason and right. This Kantian cosmopolitanism becomes a principal target of Schmitt's critique—Schmitt argues that the attempt to depoliticize international relations through universal law actually intensifies conflict by transforming enemies into criminals and just wars into wars of annihilation against evildoers.

*(Source: Kant, Immanuel. Toward Perpetual Peace and Other Writings on Politics, Peace, and History, ed. Pauline Kleingeld, trans. David L. Colclasure. Yale University Press, 2006.)*

### G.W.F. Hegel: Master-Slave Dialectic and Recognition

Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel (1770-1831) in his *Phenomenology of Spirit* (1807) develops the famous **master-slave dialectic** (Herrschaft und Knechtschaft), which profoundly influenced subsequent thinking about power, recognition, and conflict.

Hegel argues that self-consciousness requires recognition by another self-consciousness. This leads to a "life-and-death struggle" (Kampf auf Leben und Tod) in which each consciousness seeks recognition from the other. One becomes the master, the other the slave. However, this relationship proves dialectically unstable—the master depends on the slave for recognition, while the slave, through labor, develops independence.

Hegel's dialectic suggests:
- **Recognition as fundamental**: Identity and self-consciousness emerge through relationships with others, including adversarial relationships
- **Conflict as productive**: Struggle drives historical development and the emergence of higher forms of consciousness
- **The instability of domination**: Pure relations of domination contain the seeds of their own transformation

Hegel's influence extends through Marx, Kojève, and into 20th-century French philosophy. His work suggests that the friend-enemy distinction may be a permanent feature of human existence, at least until the "end of history" when full mutual recognition is achieved.

*(Source: Hegel, G.W.F. Phenomenology of Spirit, trans. A.V. Miller. Oxford University Press, 1977.)*

### Carl von Clausewitz: War as Continuation of Politics

Carl von Clausewitz (1780-1831) in *On War* (Vom Kriege) provides the most influential modern theory of warfare and its relationship to politics. His famous formulation—"War is merely the continuation of policy by other means" (Der Krieg ist eine bloße Fortsetzung der Politik mit anderen Mitteln)—establishes the subordination of military conflict to political objectives.

Clausewitz's key insights:

- **The primacy of politics**: Military force serves political purposes; war is an instrument of policy
- **The concept of total war**: War tends toward absolute violence but is restrained by political considerations
- **The friction of war**: The gap between theory and practice, plan and execution
- **The trinity**: War emerges from the interaction of passion (the people), probability (the commander), and reason (the government)

Clausewitz's work influences Schmitt's understanding of the political. The friend-enemy distinction determines when organized violence (war) becomes appropriate, and political objectives determine how that violence is employed. Schmitt sees Clausewitz as recognizing that politics always contains the possibility of war—the ultimate expression of enmity.

*(Source: Clausewitz, Carl von. On War, eds. and trans. Michael Howard and Peter Paret. Princeton University Press, 1984.)*

### Karl Marx and Friedrich Engels: Class Struggle and Antagonism

Karl Marx (1818-1883) and Friedrich Engels (1820-1895) develop a comprehensive theory of power based on economic relationships and class conflict. *The Communist Manifesto* (1848) opens with the declaration: "The history of all hitherto existing society is the history of class struggles."

Marxist analysis introduces:

- **Structural antagonism**: The friend-enemy distinction is rooted in economic structures—bourgeoisie versus proletariat
- **False consciousness**: Dominant classes use ideology to obscure true class interests
- **Revolutionary violence**: The overthrow of capitalism requires organized class struggle
- **The withering away of the state**: Post-revolutionary society will transcend political enmity through the abolition of classes

Marx's theory represents a materialist reinterpretation of Hegelian dialectics. The master-slave struggle becomes class struggle, driven by contradictions in the mode of production. The friend-enemy distinction is neither eternal nor merely political but rooted in economic relationships that can be transformed through revolutionary praxis.

Schmitt recognized the power of Marxist political theology, seeing in communist movements a revival of friend-enemy thinking that liberal capitalism sought to obscure through economic categories.

*(Source: Marx, Karl, and Friedrich Engels. The Communist Manifesto, trans. Samuel Moore. Penguin Classics, 2002.)*

### Georges Sorel: Myth, Violence, and the General Strike

Georges Sorel (1847-1922) in *Reflections on Violence* (1908) develops a theory of revolutionary violence that influenced both fascist and anarcho-syndicalist movements. Sorel argues that great historical movements require motivating myths—images of future struggle that mobilize collective action.

For Sorel, the **myth of the general strike** would galvanize the proletariat into revolutionary action. Violence, in this context, is not merely destructive but creative—it clarifies the friend-enemy distinction, sweeps away bourgeois mediations and compromises, and forces a decision.

Sorel's influence on Schmitt is evident in the latter's emphasis on decision, myth, and the rejection of liberal parliamentarism as mere talk-shop that obscures real political antagonisms. Both thinkers see liberalism as trying to avoid genuine political decision through endless discussion and economic negotiation.

*(Source: Sorel, Georges. Reflections on Violence, trans. T.E. Hulme and J. Roth. Dover Publications, 2004.)*

---

## Carl Schmitt: The Concept of the Political

### Biographical Context

Carl Schmitt (1888-1985) was a German legal and political theorist whose work profoundly influenced 20th-century political thought. His career spans the Weimar Republic, the Third Reich, and the post-war period—a trajectory that has made his work both influential and controversial.

Schmitt's most important works include:
- *Political Theology* (1922)
- *The Crisis of Parliamentary Democracy* (1923)
- *The Concept of the Political* (1927/1932)
- *Constitutional Theory* (1928)
- *Legality and Legitimacy* (1932)
- *Theory of the Partisan* (1963)
- *The Nomos of the Earth* (1950)

Schmitt's association with National Socialism after 1933, including his support for Hitler's extra-judicial killings and his advocacy for purging "Jewish influence" from German jurisprudence, has permanently tainted his legacy. Nevertheless, his theoretical insights continue to generate debate and influence contemporary political theory.

*(Source: Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy entry on Carl Schmitt, https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/schmitt/)*

### The Friend-Enemy Distinction

The opening sentence of *The Concept of the Political* announces Schmitt's central thesis: "The concept of the state presupposes the concept of the political." For Schmitt, the political cannot be defined by substantive content (morality, economics, aesthetics) but only by a specific intensity of association or dissociation.

**Schmitt's definition**: "The specific political distinction to which political actions and motives can be reduced is that between friend and enemy."

Key characteristics of this distinction:

1. **Existential, not moral**: The enemy is not necessarily evil, immoral, or aesthetically ugly. The enemy is "the other, the stranger; and it is sufficient for his nature that he is, in a specially intense way, existentially something different and alien, so that in the extreme case conflicts with him are possible."

2. **Public, not private**: The political enemy is a public enemy (hostis), not a private adversary (inimicus). The distinction concerns groups, not individuals.

3. **Extreme case—possibility of war**: The friend-enemy distinction implies the real possibility of physical killing. War is the extreme manifestation of enmity.

4. **Autonomous sphere**: The political is not reducible to other antitheses (good/evil, beautiful/ugly, profitable/unprofitable). Any distinction can become political if it is intense enough to group people into friend and enemy.

5. **Self-determination**: Only the political entity itself can decide who is friend and who is enemy. This decision cannot be delegated to a third party without losing political sovereignty.

*(Source: Schmitt, Carl. The Concept of the Political. Expanded Edition, trans. George Schwab. University of Chicago Press, 2007.)*

### Critique of Liberalism

Schmitt's friend-enemy distinction provides the basis for a comprehensive critique of liberalism. According to Schmitt, liberalism attempts to avoid the political through:

- **Economization**: Reducing political conflicts to commercial negotiations and economic exchanges
- **Juridification**: Transforming political decisions into legal questions subject to judicial resolution
- **Parliamentarism**: Endless discussion that postpones decision and obscures real antagonisms
- **Humanitarianism**: Universal moral categories that deny the legitimacy of political enmity

Schmitt argues that liberal depoliticization is both impossible and dangerous:

**Impossible** because:
- The friend-enemy distinction will always reassert itself
- Economic and moral conflicts can escalate to political intensity
- Political existence requires the capacity to distinguish friend from enemy

**Dangerous** because:
- Humanitarian rhetoric transforms enemies into criminals, making war more total
- Denial of political enmity leads to civil war when conflicts become intense
- Economic imperialism operates under false universalism

The liberal state, according to Schmitt, becomes a Rechtsstaat (state based on law) that cannot maintain political unity when faced with genuine existential threats.

*(Source: Schmitt, Carl. The Crisis of Parliamentary Democracy, trans. Ellen Kennedy. MIT Press, 1985.)*

### Sovereignty and the Exception

In *Political Theology*, Schmitt offers his famous definition: "Sovereign is he who decides on the exception." This formulation connects sovereignty to the friend-enemy distinction through the concept of the existential decision.

The exception reveals:
- **The limits of legal normality**: Law presupposes a "normal situation" but cannot determine when that normality exists
- **The necessity of decision**: Someone must decide whether an emergency exists and what measures to take
- **The foundation of legal order**: The exception reveals that legal order rests on a decision that is not itself legally regulated

Schmitt distinguishes between:
- **Commissarial dictatorship**: Temporary suspension of law to restore normal constitutional order
- **Sovereign dictatorship**: Creation of a new constitutional order through constituent power

The sovereign decision on the exception allows the state to suspend law to defend itself against existential threats—those who threaten the state's form of existence are revealed as enemies who can be treated outside the normal legal order.

This concept anticipates later developments such as Giorgio Agamben's "state of exception" and contemporary debates about emergency powers, terrorism, and the suspension of civil liberties.

*(Source: Schmitt, Carl. Political Theology: Four Chapters on the Concept of Sovereignty, trans. George Schwab. University of Chicago Press, 2005.)*

### Democracy and Homogeneity

Schmitt develops a controversial theory of democracy based on identity rather than representation. True democracy, for Schmitt, requires **substantial equality** among citizens—a homogeneity based on shared identity that distinguishes members from non-members.

Key propositions:

- **Democracy = identity of ruler and ruled**: The people govern themselves directly, not through representatives
- **Homogeneity requirement**: Democratic equality applies only within a homogeneous group
- **Exclusion as necessary**: Democracy requires the ability to distinguish between members and non-members, citizens and foreigners
- **Acclamation over deliberation**: The people express their will through plebiscite and acclamation, not parliamentary debate

Schmitt's democratic theory leads to troubling conclusions:
- Immigration must be controlled to preserve homogeneity
- Internal enemies may need to be suppressed or eliminated
- Liberal protections for minorities undermine democratic unity
- Parliamentary discussion obscures the real popular will

This identitarian conception of democracy influenced both fascist movements and later populist theories. Schmitt explicitly supported Hitler's rise to power, arguing that the Nazi movement represented an authentic expression of the German people's constituent power.

*(Source: Schmitt, Carl. Constitutional Theory, trans. Jeffrey Seitzer. Duke University Press, 2008.)*

### International Order and the Nomos of the Earth

In *The Nomos of the Earth* (written during WWII, published 1950), Schmitt develops his theory of international order based on spatial ordering (nomos) and the friend-enemy distinction between states.

Schmitt argues that legitimate international order requires:

1. **Pluralism of sovereign states**: No universal authority can eliminate political enmity
2. **Spatial divisions**: Clear territorial boundaries that allow spatialization of conflicts
3. **Mutual recognition**: States recognize each other as legitimate enemies (justus hostis)
4. **Distinction between ius ad bellum and ius in bello**: States have sovereign right to wage war, but war is limited by rules governing conduct

Schmitt praises the **jus publicum Europaeum** (European public law) of the 17th-19th centuries, which he claims bracketed warfare through:
- Recognition of state sovereignty
- Limitation of war to state conflicts (not wars of annihilation)
- Clear distinction between combatants and civilians
- Right to neutrality

Schmitt argues that 20th-century attempts to criminalize aggressive war (League of Nations, UN Charter) actually make war more total by denying legitimacy to the enemy. When enemies become criminals rather than legitimate adversaries, war becomes a war of annihilation for the triumph of good over evil.

This theory led Schmitt to justify Nazi aggression as the establishment of German *Großraum* (large space) analogous to the American Monroe Doctrine. Post-war, he used the same framework to criticize American hegemony and the liberal internationalist order.

*(Source: Schmitt, Carl. The Nomos of the Earth in the International Law of the Jus Publicum Europaeum, trans. G.L. Ulmen. Telos Press, 2003.)*

### Schmitt's Theological Foundations

Throughout his work, Schmitt emphasizes the theological roots of political concepts. In *Political Theology*, he declares: "All significant concepts of the modern theory of the state are secularized theological concepts."

Schmitt's political theology includes:

- **Original sin**: Humanity's fallen nature makes conflict permanent and peace impossible in the temporal world
- **Katechon**: The "restraining force" (2 Thessalonians 2:6-7) that holds back the Antichrist—for Schmitt, political authority that preserves order against chaos
- **The friend-enemy distinction as inscribed in humanity**: Enmity as a consequence of the Fall and Cain's murder of Abel
- **The impossibility of universal peace**: Only God can finally eliminate enmity at the end of history

This theological framework suggests that liberal cosmopolitanism represents a form of hubris—an attempt to achieve through human effort what only divine intervention can accomplish. The denial of political enmity becomes a rebellion against the Christian understanding of human nature and history.

Critics argue that Schmitt's political theology provides a dangerous legitimation for authoritarian power and political violence, dressing power politics in theological garb.

*(Sources: Schmitt, Carl. Political Theology; Mehring, Reinhard. Carl Schmitt: A Biography, trans. Daniel Steuer. Polity Press, 2014; Balakrishnan, Gopal. The Enemy: An Intellectual Portrait of Carl Schmitt. Verso, 2000.)*

---

## Friedrich Nietzsche: Will to Power and Agonal Existence

### Nietzsche's Critique of Morality

Friedrich Nietzsche (1844-1900) provides philosophical foundations for rethinking power and enmity outside traditional moral categories. His critique of morality, developed across works like *Beyond Good and Evil* (1886), *On the Genealogy of Morals* (1887), and *Thus Spoke Zarathustra* (1883-1885), challenges the Christian and liberal moral frameworks that condemn power and conflict.

Nietzsche's genealogical method reveals:

- **Master morality vs. slave morality**: The former affirms power, strength, nobility; the latter resents the strong and elevates weakness as virtue
- **Ressentiment**: The slave revolt in morality is driven by resentment of the powerful, transforming natural inequality into moral evil
- **The inversion of values**: Christianity represents the triumph of slave morality, demonizing natural human instincts including the capacity for enmity

For Nietzsche, traditional morality is a form of power exercised by the weak against the strong—a more insidious form of power that operates through guilt, bad conscience, and ascetic ideals.

*(Source: Nietzsche, Friedrich. On the Genealogy of Morals, trans. Walter Kaufmann. Vintage Books, 1989.)*

### The Will to Power

Central to Nietzsche's philosophy is the concept of **will to power** (Wille zur Macht)—the fundamental drive of all life to enhance and overcome itself. Will to power is:

- **Not merely domination**: It encompasses creativity, growth, self-overcoming, and the imposition of form
- **Universal**: Present in all living things, from the simplest organisms to complex human psychology
- **Agonal**: Expressed through contest, struggle, and the testing of strength

In *Beyond Good and Evil*, Nietzsche writes: "Life itself is essentially appropriation, injury, overpowering of what is alien and weaker; suppression, hardness, imposition of one's own forms, incorporation and at least, at its mildest, exploitation."

This naturalistic account of power challenges liberal humanitarian assumptions that conflict and domination can or should be eliminated. For Nietzsche, they are inherent to life itself.

*(Source: Nietzsche, Friedrich. Beyond Good and Evil, trans. Walter Kaufmann. Vintage Books, 1989.)*

### The Friend as Best Enemy

In *Thus Spoke Zarathustra*, Zarathustra proclaims: "In a friend one should have one's best enemy. You should be closest to him with your heart when you resist him."

This paradoxical formulation suggests:

- **Agonal friendship**: True friends challenge each other, providing worthy opposition that enables growth
- **Contest as respect**: Opposition is a form of recognition—only those one respects are worthy enemies
- **Beyond good and evil**: Friend and enemy cannot be mapped onto moral categories of good and evil

Nietzsche's aristocratic ideal celebrates:
- Agonal contest (like ancient Greek athletics and warfare)
- The pathos of distance that maintains tension and drive for excellence
- Friendship as a form of shared nobility that includes struggle

This view contrasts with both Schmitt's existential enmity (which threatens annihilation) and liberal humanitarianism (which seeks to eliminate conflict). For Nietzsche, a world without enemies would be a world without excellence or growth.

*(Source: Nietzsche, Friedrich. Thus Spoke Zarathustra, trans. Walter Kaufmann. Viking Penguin, 1966.)*

### The Eternal Return and Amor Fati

Nietzsche's doctrine of **eternal return**—the idea that all events recur infinitely—provides a test for life-affirmation. Could you will the eternal recurrence of your life exactly as it is, including all suffering, all enemies, all conflicts?

To say "yes" to eternal return requires **amor fati** (love of fate)—affirming life in its entirety, including its agonal nature. This includes affirming:
- The conflicts and enemies that shaped you
- The struggles that produced greatness
- The tragic dimension of existence

Nietzsche's affirmation of life's agonal character stands opposed to:
- Christian otherworldliness that denies this life in favor of the next
- Liberal progressivism that believes conflict can be rationally eliminated
- Pessimistic resignation that sees only meaningless suffering

*(Source: Nietzsche, Friedrich. The Gay Science, trans. Walter Kaufmann. Vintage Books, 1974.)*

### The Übermensch and Self-Overcoming

Nietzsche's **Übermensch** (Overman/Superman) represents humanity's potential for self-overcoming—transcending current human limitations to create new values. The Übermensch:

- Creates values rather than following inherited morality
- Affirms life including its agonal and tragic dimensions
- Transforms suffering and conflict into sources of strength
- Represents humanity's highest possibility

The path to the Übermensch requires overcoming:
- The "last man" who seeks only comfort and the elimination of danger
- Slave morality that denies life's power dynamics
- The spirit of revenge and ressentiment

Power, for Nietzsche, ultimately means the power of self-creation and self-overcoming. The greatest enemies may be internal—aspects of ourselves we must overcome to achieve our highest potential.

*(Source: Nietzsche, Friedrich. Thus Spoke Zarathustra)*

### Influence on Schmitt and Fascism

Nietzsche's philosophy influenced various 20th-century political movements, often in distorted forms. His critique of liberalism, emphasis on power, celebration of nobility and hierarchy, and cultural pessimism made him attractive to:

- Conservative revolutionaries in Weimar Germany (including Schmitt)
- Italian Fascists who celebrated vitalism and struggle
- Various right-wing movements seeking alternatives to liberal democracy

However, Nietzsche himself remained deeply ambivalent about politics:
- He criticized nationalism and anti-Semitism
- He disdained mass movements and the state
- He advocated for "free spirits" and individual excellence over collective identity

The relationship between Nietzsche and Schmitt is complex. Both critique liberal rationalism and recognize the ineliminability of conflict, but:
- Nietzsche focuses on individual self-overcoming; Schmitt on collective political identity
- Nietzsche's enemies are internal (aspects of self to overcome); Schmitt's are external (threats to political existence)
- Nietzsche seeks to transcend good and evil; Schmitt grounds politics in the friend-enemy distinction

*(Sources: Aydin, Ciano. The Struggle Between Ideals: Nietzsche, Schmitt and the Politics of Enmity. University of Twente, 2008; Appel, Frederick. Nietzsche Contra Democracy. Cornell University Press, 1999.)*

---

## 20th Century Developments

### Max Weber: Legitimate Violence and the State

Max Weber (1864-1920) provides a foundational sociological account of power and the state in "Politics as a Vocation" (1919). Weber's famous definition: "The state is a human community that (successfully) claims the monopoly of the legitimate use of physical force within a given territory."

Key Weberian concepts:

- **Monopoly on legitimate violence**: The modern state is defined by its exclusive right to use force
- **Three types of legitimate authority**: Traditional, charismatic, and legal-rational
- **Bureaucratization**: Modern states increasingly rely on rational-legal authority implemented through bureaucracy
- **Politics as vocation**: Political leaders must be able to use violence when necessary—an ethic of responsibility that acknowledges the tragic dimension of politics

Weber's analysis of the state as the institution that determines the legitimate use of violence directly relates to the friend-enemy distinction. The state decides against whom violence may be legitimately employed—who is the enemy.

Weber's pessimism about the **iron cage of bureaucracy** and the rationalization of modern life influenced later conservative critics of mass democracy and liberalism, including Schmitt.

*(Source: Weber, Max. "Politics as a Vocation," in From Max Weber: Essays in Sociology, eds. H.H. Gerth and C. Wright Mills. Oxford University Press, 1946.)*

### Michel Foucault: Power/Knowledge and Biopower

Michel Foucault (1926-1984) revolutionized thinking about power in works like *Discipline and Punish* (1975) and *The History of Sexuality* (1976-1984). Foucault's analytics of power moves beyond sovereignty and repression to analyze how power operates through knowledge, discourse, and the production of subjectivity.

Key Foucauldian concepts:

- **Power/knowledge**: Power and knowledge are mutually constitutive—power produces knowledge, knowledge enables power
- **Disciplinary power**: Modern power operates through surveillance, normalization, and the production of "docile bodies"
- **Biopower**: Modern states govern populations through management of life itself—health, sexuality, birth rates, etc.
- **Governmentality**: The techniques and rationalities through which populations are governed

Foucault's approach challenges Schmitt in important ways:

- Power is not primarily juridical (about law and sovereignty) but productive (creating subjects and knowledge)
- Power operates not through the friend-enemy distinction but through normalization and biopolitics
- Resistance is always possible within power relations—power is not simply repressive

However, Foucault also recognizes that biopower can produce its own form of enmity—populations deemed dangerous, abnormal, or contaminating may be subjected to exclusion, normalization, or even extermination. The Nazi regime represents, for Foucault, the murderous intersection of sovereign power and biopower.

*(Sources: Foucault, Michel. Discipline and Punish: The Birth of the Prison, trans. Alan Sheridan. Vintage Books, 1995; Foucault, Michel. The History of Sexuality, Volume 1, trans. Robert Hurley. Vintage Books, 1990; Foucault, Michel. Society Must Be Defended: Lectures at the Collège de France 1975-1976, trans. David Macey. Picador, 2003.)*

### Hannah Arendt: Totalitarianism, Violence, and Power

Hannah Arendt (1906-1975) provides crucial analyses of totalitarianism, the nature of power, and the relationship between violence and politics. In *The Origins of Totalitarianism* (1951), Arendt traces how total domination becomes possible through:

- **The destruction of political space**: Totalitarian movements eliminate the plurality essential to politics
- **Radical evil**: The attempt to make human beings "superfluous" through terror
- **Ideological thinking**: Pseudo-scientific ideologies (race, class) that provide the friend-enemy logic justifying terror

In *On Violence* (1970), Arendt makes a crucial distinction:

**Power ≠ Violence**

- **Power**: Emerges from people acting together; depends on consent and legitimacy
- **Violence**: Instrumental; destroys power rather than creating it
- **Authority**: Recognized right to lead; doesn't require coercion

This analysis challenges Schmitt's friend-enemy framework:
- True political power requires plurality and debate, not homogeneity
- Violence is antipolitical—it destroys the space for politics
- The friend-enemy distinction leads toward totalitarianism, not genuine politics

Arendt's work on totalitarianism shows the catastrophic consequences of politics based purely on friend-enemy logic—the Nazi identification of Jews as racial enemies led to the Holocaust, the attempt to eliminate an entire people from the earth.

*(Sources: Arendt, Hannah. The Origins of Totalitarianism. Harcourt Brace Jovanovich, 1973; Arendt, Hannah. On Violence. Harcourt, 1970; Arendt, Hannah. The Human Condition. University of Chicago Press, 1958.)*

### Leo Strauss: Critique of Schmitt

Leo Strauss (1899-1973), German-Jewish political philosopher who fled Nazi Germany, engaged critically with Schmitt's work in his "Notes on Carl Schmitt's *Concept of the Political*" (1932). Despite their mutual respect, Strauss identified fundamental problems:

**Strauss's critique**:

1. **The horizon of liberalism**: Schmitt's critique remains within the liberal horizon—he assumes the value of peace and order even while claiming to affirm the political
2. **The problem of the moral question**: Why should we affirm the political? Schmitt avoids this moral question
3. **The relationship to philosophy**: Schmitt's anti-rationalism makes his position philosophically untenable
4. **The status of truth**: If there is truth about right order, the friend-enemy distinction cannot be ultimate

Strauss argues that Schmitt's rejection of liberal universalism leads him toward a dangerous particularism that cannot justify itself philosophically. Yet Strauss also recognizes the force of Schmitt's critique of liberalism.

The Strauss-Schmitt exchange illustrates tensions between:
- Philosophy (concerned with truth and justice) and politics (concerned with friend and enemy)
- Ancient political philosophy and modern political theology
- Jerusalem (revelation) and Athens (philosophy)

*(Sources: Strauss, Leo. "Notes on Carl Schmitt's Concept of the Political," trans. J. Harvey Lomax, in Carl Schmitt, The Concept of the Political, Expanded Edition. University of Chicago Press, 2007; Meier, Heinrich. Carl Schmitt and Leo Strauss: The Hidden Dialogue, trans. J. Harvey Lomax. University of Chicago Press, 2006.)*

### Alexandre Kojève: Recognition and the End of History

Alexandre Kojève (1902-1968), Russian-French philosopher, delivered influential lectures on Hegel's *Phenomenology* in Paris (1933-1939) that shaped a generation of French intellectuals. Kojève's interpretation of Hegel's master-slave dialectic emphasized:

- **The struggle for recognition** (Anerkennung): Human history is driven by the desire for recognition by other self-consciousnesses
- **Master and slave**: The life-and-death struggle produces relations of domination but is dialectically unstable
- **The end of history**: Modern liberal democracy represents the end of history—universal recognition eliminates the need for further struggle

Kojève argued that post-WWII, history had essentially ended—the struggle between master and slave, between competing ideologies, had been resolved in favor of liberal democratic capitalism. The friend-enemy distinction would gradually dissolve as all humanity achieved mutual recognition.

Francis Fukuyama would later popularize this thesis in *The End of History and the Last Man* (1992), arguing that liberal democracy represents the endpoint of ideological evolution.

This optimistic vision stands in stark contrast to Schmitt's conviction that the political (and thus the friend-enemy distinction) is ineliminable. The late 20th and early 21st centuries have not vindicated Kojève's thesis—political theology, nationalism, and ideological conflict have returned forcefully.

*(Source: Kojève, Alexandre. Introduction to the Reading of Hegel: Lectures on the Phenomenology of Spirit, ed. Allan Bloom, trans. James H. Nichols Jr. Cornell University Press, 1980.)*

### Giorgio Agamben: Homo Sacer and State of Exception

Giorgio Agamben (1942-) builds on Schmitt, Foucault, and Walter Benjamin to develop a comprehensive critique of sovereignty and biopower. His *Homo Sacer* series (1995-) argues:

**The state of exception has become the rule**:

- Modern sovereignty operates through the power to suspend law
- The "state of exception" (Schmitt's exception) is no longer exceptional—it defines normal politics
- The concentration camp becomes the nomos (spatial ordering) of modernity

**Homo sacer** (sacred man):

- A figure from Roman law who could be killed but not sacrificed
- Represents "bare life" (zoē) stripped of political existence (bios)
- The refugee, the detainee, the stateless person as modern homo sacer

Agamben's work reveals the dark potential of Schmitt's friend-enemy logic:
- Those identified as enemies can be reduced to bare life
- Sovereign power decides who lives and who dies
- The state of exception produces zones (camps, black sites) where law is suspended

Agamben's analysis applies to contemporary phenomena:
- Guantanamo Bay and extraordinary rendition
- Refugee camps and border zones
- Targeted killing and drone warfare
- COVID-19 emergency measures

*(Sources: Agamben, Giorgio. Homo Sacer: Sovereign Power and Bare Life, trans. Daniel Heller-Roazen. Stanford University Press, 1998; Agamben, Giorgio. State of Exception, trans. Kevin Attell. University of Chicago Press, 2005.)*

### Chantal Mouffe: Agonistic Democracy

Chantal Mouffe (1943-), Belgian political theorist, engages productively with Schmitt to develop an "agonistic" model of democracy. In *The Democratic Paradox* (2000) and *Agonistics* (2013), Mouffe argues:

**Against liberal consensus**:

- Liberal democracy's attempt to eliminate conflict is both impossible and undesirable
- "Consensus without exclusion" is a dangerous illusion
- Politics always involves power, conflict, and decisions

**Agonism vs. antagonism**:

- **Antagonism** (Schmitt): Friend-enemy relation involving the possibility of violence
- **Agonism** (Mouffe): Adversaries within a shared democratic framework
- Democratic institutions should channel conflict into agonistic contest rather than attempting to eliminate it

**Radical democracy**:

- Recognizes that democracy requires both liberty and equality
- Pluralism of values means conflict cannot be rationally resolved
- Democratic contestation must remain open—no final solution

Mouffe's agonistic democracy accepts Schmitt's critique of liberal rationalism while rejecting his friend-enemy logic. Adversaries can contest intensely within democratic bounds without treating each other as existential enemies to be eliminated.

Critics argue that Mouffe's distinction between antagonism and agonism is unstable—intense political conflict tends to escalate into existential enmity, as Schmitt recognized.

*(Sources: Mouffe, Chantal. The Democratic Paradox. Verso, 2000; Mouffe, Chantal. Agonistics: Thinking the World Politically. Verso, 2013; Mouffe, Chantal, ed. The Challenge of Carl Schmitt. Verso, 1999.)*

### Jacques Derrida: Deconstruction and Politics of Friendship

Jacques Derrida (1930-2004) in *Politics of Friendship* (1994) deconstructs the friend-enemy distinction, revealing its instabilities and aporias. Derrida analyzes the Greek maxim "O my friends, there is no friend," attributed to Aristotle, to explore:

**The impossibility and necessity of friendship**:

- Pure friendship (without self-interest, calculation, or enemy) may be impossible
- Yet we cannot abandon the concept of friendship
- Politics requires thinking community without reducing it to a logic of brother-enemy

**Hospitality**:

- Absolute hospitality (welcoming the stranger without conditions) is impossible but necessary as a regulative ideal
- Conditional hospitality (welcoming under certain conditions) is necessary but betrays pure hospitality
- This aporia structures our relationship to the Other and the enemy

Derrida's deconstructive approach challenges Schmitt:
- The friend-enemy distinction is not as stable or clear as Schmitt suggests
- The Other cannot be simply identified as friend or enemy
- Justice requires a relationship to the Other that exceeds calculation and the friend-enemy logic

However, Derrida also recognizes that we cannot simply dispense with decisions—deconstruction does not provide a simple alternative to Schmitt's decisionism.

*(Source: Derrida, Jacques. Politics of Friendship, trans. George Collins. Verso, 1997.)*

### Antonio Gramsci: Hegemony and Cultural Power

Antonio Gramsci (1891-1937), Italian Marxist, developed the concept of **hegemony** in his *Prison Notebooks* to explain how dominant classes maintain power not merely through coercion but through cultural leadership and consent.

Key concepts:

- **Hegemony**: Cultural and ideological dominance that makes power appear natural and legitimate
- **Organic intellectuals**: Those who articulate the worldview of a class or group
- **War of position vs. war of maneuver**: In developed societies, revolutionary strategy requires a cultural war of position, not merely frontal assault on state power

Gramsci's analysis complicates the friend-enemy distinction:
- Power operates through consent and culture, not merely force
- The ruling class constructs common sense and shapes what appears possible
- Counter-hegemony requires building alternative cultural and intellectual frameworks

Gramsci influenced later thinkers including Stuart Hall, Edward Said, and the Birmingham School of cultural studies—all analyzing how power operates through cultural representation and the construction of "normal" versus "deviant" or "enemy."

*(Source: Gramsci, Antonio. Selections from the Prison Notebooks, eds. and trans. Quintin Hoare and Geoffrey Nowell Smith. International Publishers, 1971.)*

---

## Contemporary Applications

### The War on Terror and Schmittian Politics

The post-9/11 "War on Terror" involved a return to Schmittian concepts:

**State of exception**:

- Emergency powers and suspension of normal legal protections
- Military detention without trial (Guantanamo Bay)
- Extraordinary rendition and black sites
- Surveillance without warrants

**Friend-enemy identification**:

- "You're either with us or with the terrorists" (George W. Bush)
- Designation of "enemy combatants" outside Geneva Conventions
- Targeted killing programs (drones)
- Preventive detention and preventive war

**Schmittian critiques were deployed by both left and right**:

- **Left/civil libertarians**: Warned of permanent emergency, erosion of rights, and executive overreach
- **Right/national security advocates**: Argued that existential threats justify Schmittian decisionism

The debate over torture exemplified Schmittian logic:
- Alan Dershowitz's "torture warrants": Formalizing the exception
- Critics: Once you normalize torture, the exception becomes the rule
- Defenders: Ticking time bomb scenarios require sovereign decision beyond law

*(Sources: Agamben, Giorgio. State of Exception; Dyzenhaus, David. The Constitution of Law: Legality in a Time of Emergency. Cambridge University Press, 2006; Posner, Eric A., and Adrian Vermeule. The Executive Unbound: After the Madisonian Republic. Oxford University Press, 2010.)*

### Populism and the Friend-Enemy Logic

Contemporary populist movements in Europe and the Americas have revived friend-enemy politics:

**Right-wing populism**:

- Identification of elites, immigrants, Muslims, or "globalists" as enemies
- Construction of "the people" as a homogeneous entity
- Critique of liberal institutions as obstacles to the popular will
- Charismatic leaders who claim to embody the people's will

**Left-wing populism**:

- Identification of financial elites, neoliberalism, or "the 1%" as enemies
- Construction of "the 99%" as the people
- Critique of technocratic governance divorced from popular control
- Movements like Podemos (Spain) and Syriza (Greece)

Both forms employ Schmittian logic:
- Politics as friend-enemy conflict, not consensus or deliberation
- Rejection of liberal pluralism in favor of popular sovereignty
- Skepticism toward mediating institutions and expertise
- Appeal to decisive action against enemies of the people

The rise of populism has generated debates about "democratic backsliding" and whether liberal democracy can survive without eliminating the friend-enemy distinction that Schmitt saw as essential to politics.

*(Sources: Müller, Jan-Werner. What Is Populism? University of Pennsylvania Press, 2016; Mouffe, Chantal. For a Left Populism. Verso, 2018; Mudde, Cas, and Cristóbal Rovira Kaltwasser. Populism: A Very Short Introduction. Oxford University Press, 2017.)*

### Identity Politics and Political Polarization

Contemporary identity politics involves complex friend-enemy dynamics:

**Progressive identity politics**:

- Recognition of structural oppression along lines of race, gender, sexuality, etc.
- Identification of systems of oppression as enemies (white supremacy, patriarchy, heteronormativity)
- Emphasis on standpoint epistemology and lived experience
- Calls for solidarity among oppressed groups

**Conservative reaction**:

- Critique of "political correctness" and "cancel culture" as tyranny of the minority
- Defense of traditional values and identities perceived as under attack
- Identification of progressive movements as enemies of national identity, free speech, or Western civilization

Both sides employ friend-enemy logic:
- Drawing sharp boundaries between acceptable and unacceptable views
- Identifying enemies whose positions place them beyond the pale
- Difficulty finding common ground or shared premises for debate

This polarization has led to concerns about:
- Balkanization of public discourse
- Inability to deliberate across difference
- Threat of political violence or civil conflict
- Breakdown of liberal norms of toleration

The friend-enemy distinction operates not only between political parties but across multiple axes of identity and ideology, creating complex and shifting alliance structures reminiscent of Kautilya's Mandala theory.

*(Sources: Lilla, Mark. The Once and Future Liberal: After Identity Politics. Harper, 2017; Fukuyama, Francis. Identity: The Demand for Dignity and the Politics of Resentment. Farrar, Straus and Giroux, 2018; Taylor, Charles. Multiculturalism and "The Politics of Recognition." Princeton University Press, 1992.)*

### Climate Change as New Friend-Enemy Divide?

Climate change presents interesting challenges to the friend-enemy distinction:

**Potential for new enmities**:

- Climate deniers as enemies of human survival
- Fossil fuel companies as enemies of the planet
- Developed nations as enemies of developing nations
- Future generations as friends whose interests current generations betray

**Schmittian concerns**:

- Environmentalism as depoliticization through technocracy
- Climate emergency as justification for state of exception
- Authoritarian climate governance ("eco-fascism")
- Climate lockdowns and restrictions on liberty

**Alternative: Common enemy**:

- Climate change as common threat that transcends friend-enemy politics
- Humanity united against environmental collapse
- New forms of solidarity and cooperation

However, Schmitt would likely argue that even facing a common natural threat, humans will disagree about responses, creating new friend-enemy divisions over climate policy, economic systems, and power distribution.

The COVID-19 pandemic similarly revealed how even shared threats generate friend-enemy dynamics around:
- Public health measures vs. individual liberty
- International cooperation vs. vaccine nationalism
- Scientific expertise vs. popular sovereignty

*(Sources: Malm, Andreas. How to Blow Up a Pipeline: Learning to Fight in a World on Fire. Verso, 2021; Mann, Geoff, and Joel Wainwright. Climate Leviathan: A Political Theory of Our Planetary Future. Verso, 2018; Latour, Bruno. Down to Earth: Politics in the New Climatic Regime, trans. Catherine Porter. Polity Press, 2018.)*

### Technological Power and Surveillance

Contemporary technology creates new forms of power that complicate friend-enemy distinctions:

**Algorithmic governance**:

- Automated decision-making about risk, creditworthiness, dangerousness
- Predictive policing and pre-crime
- Social credit systems
- Content moderation and deplatforming

**Surveillance capitalism** (Shoshana Zuboff):

- Extraction of behavioral data as new form of power
- Prediction and modification of behavior for profit
- Asymmetric knowledge and power relations

**Questions for friend-enemy politics**:

- Can algorithms identify enemies?
- Does surveillance create a permanent state of exception?
- How do we exercise political agency against algorithmic power?
- Is "tech industry" an enemy to be opposed or neutral tool to be regulated?

The distributed, non-territorial nature of digital power challenges Schmitt's emphasis on spatial ordering and clear boundaries. Cyberwarfare and information warfare create enemies who cannot be clearly located or definitively identified.

*(Sources: Zuboff, Shoshana. The Age of Surveillance Capitalism: The Fight for a Human Future at the New Frontier of Power. PublicAffairs, 2019; Pasquale, Frank. The Black Box Society: The Secret Algorithms That Control Money and Information. Harvard University Press, 2015; Morozov, Evgeny. To Save Everything, Click Here: The Folly of Technological Solutionism. PublicAffairs, 2013.)*

### The Amity-Enmity Complex in Social Psychology

Modern social psychology has explored the friend-enemy distinction empirically:

**In-group/out-group dynamics**:

- Minimal group paradigm (Henri Tajfel): Even arbitrary group assignments create in-group preference and out-group hostility
- Social identity theory: Self-esteem partly derived from group membership
- Realistic conflict theory: Competition for resources produces intergroup hostility

**The enmity paradox** (mathematical sociology):

- Network analysis shows individuals' enemies tend to have more enemies than they do
- Complex dynamics of enmity propagation through social networks
- Implications for understanding polarization and conflict escalation

**Evolutionary psychology perspectives**:

- Sir Arthur Keith's "amity-enmity complex": Human evolution favored in-group cooperation paired with out-group hostility
- Tribalism as evolved psychological mechanism
- "Parochial altruism": Cooperation within groups enables competition between groups

These findings suggest Schmitt may have identified a deep feature of human social psychology, though they don't necessarily justify his normative conclusions about politics.

*(Sources: Keith, Sir Arthur. A New Theory of Human Evolution. Philosophical Library, 1949; Tajfel, Henri, et al. "Social Categorization and Intergroup Behaviour." European Journal of Social Psychology 1.2 (1971): 149-178; The Enmity Paradox study available at arXiv:2304.10076)*

### Post-Colonial Theory and the Construction of the Other

Post-colonial theory reveals how friend-enemy distinctions operate through colonial and racial hierarchies:

**Edward Said's *Orientalism***:

- The "Orient" as constructed by Western discourse
- The Oriental as enemy, inferior, dangerous, exotic Other
- Power operates through representation and knowledge production

**Frantz Fanon's *The Wretched of the Earth***:

- Colonizer-colonized relation as existential enmity
- Violence as necessary for liberation and self-constitution
- Recognition through revolutionary struggle

**Gayatri Spivak's subaltern studies**:

- Can the subaltern speak within systems that define them as Other?
- Epistemic violence and the silencing of alternative voices

Post-colonial critique reveals that the friend-enemy distinction is not neutral or natural but constructed through relations of power, often along racial, civilizational, or religious lines. The "War on Terror" echoed colonial constructions of the dangerous Other requiring civilizing intervention.

*(Sources: Said, Edward W. Orientalism. Pantheon Books, 1978; Fanon, Frantz. The Wretched of the Earth, trans. Richard Philcox. Grove Press, 2004; Spivak, Gayatri Chakravorty. "Can the Subaltern Speak?" in Marxism and the Interpretation of Culture, eds. Cary Nelson and Lawrence Grossberg. University of Illinois Press, 1988.)*

---

## Mechanisms of Friend-Enemy Distinction: How It Actually Operates

Having traced the intellectual history of the friend-enemy distinction, we must now examine the concrete mechanisms through which this distinction operates in political life. This section analyzes not merely what thinkers have said about the distinction, but how it functions practically—the social, psychological, institutional, and symbolic processes that create, maintain, and transform boundaries between friend and enemy.

### The Production of Enmity: Mechanisms of Othering

The friend-enemy distinction is not natural or self-evident but must be actively produced and maintained through various mechanisms:

**1. Symbolic Marking and Naming**

The first mechanism involves naming the enemy and attaching symbolic markers that distinguish them from friends. This operates through:

- **Dehumanizing language**: Describing enemies as animals (rats, cockroaches, parasites), diseases (cancer, virus, plague), or inhuman entities (monsters, demons, savages). The Nazi designation of Jews as *Ungeziefer* (vermin) facilitated genocide by removing moral barriers to extermination. *(Arendt, Origins of Totalitarianism)*

- **Essentializing identities**: Reducing complex human beings to a single identity category that defines them as dangerous—"the Jew," "the terrorist," "the communist," "the infidel." This process transforms individuals into instances of a category, enabling collective treatment. *(Said, Orientalism)*

- **Inversion of moral valence**: What the enemy does is fundamentally evil while identical actions by one's own side are justified. Killing becomes "murder" when they do it, "defense" when we do it. *(Schmitt notes this moral-political distinction in Concept of the Political)*

- **Metonymic substitution**: Making particular enemies stand for broader categories. "Saddam Hussein = Iraq = Arab = Muslim = terrorist" creates chains of equivalence that expand the category of enemy. *(Laclau and Mouffe's discourse theory)*

**2. Boundary Maintenance Rituals**

Political communities perform rituals that enact and reinforce friend-enemy boundaries:

- **Loyalty oaths and pledges**: Requiring declarations of allegiance that simultaneously identify enemies by exclusion. The US Pledge of Allegiance, Soviet loyalty oaths, religious professions of faith all mark boundaries.

- **Commemorative practices**: Memorial days, monuments, and rituals that honor friends killed by enemies inscribe enmity into collective memory. Veterans Day, Holocaust Memorial Day, 9/11 commemorations maintain awareness of past and present enemies.

- **Citizenship ceremonies**: Naturalizations that transform aliens into citizens through ritualized declarations often include explicit renunciation of foreign allegiances—marking the previous status as enemy territory.

- **Purges and show trials**: Dramatic public proceedings that identify internal enemies and expel them from the community. Stalin's show trials, McCarthy hearings, Cultural Revolution struggle sessions all perform boundary maintenance through violence and humiliation.

**3. Institutional Mechanisms of Exclusion**

Friend-enemy distinctions become embedded in institutional structures:

- **Border controls and walls**: Physical barriers (Berlin Wall, Israeli West Bank barrier, US-Mexico border wall) materialize the friend-enemy distinction in space. Borders don't just mark territory—they embody political enmity.

- **Security services**: Institutions dedicated to identifying and monitoring enemies—secret police, intelligence agencies, counter-terrorism units. Their existence presupposes the reality of internal and external enemies.

- **Immigration and citizenship law**: Legal frameworks that codify who can belong and who must be excluded. US Chinese Exclusion Act, Nazi Nuremberg Laws, contemporary "Muslim bans" legally instantiate friend-enemy distinctions.

- **Military organizations**: Armed forces structured around the possibility of killing enemies. Military training involves deliberate dehumanization of potential enemies to overcome moral prohibitions against killing.

- **Enemy combatant status**: Legal categories that place individuals outside normal legal protections. Guantanamo detainees, "unlawful combatants," martial law provisions create zones where law is suspended for enemies. *(Agamben, State of Exception)*

**4. Narratives of Identity and Threat**

Stories that constitute political identity through opposition:

- **Foundation myths**: National narratives often center on existential conflicts with enemies—American Revolution against British tyranny, French Revolution against aristocratic enemies of the people, Israeli founding against Arab rejection.

- **Conspiracy theories**: Narratives that explain social problems through hidden enemy actions. "Jewish conspiracy," "Communist infiltration," "globalist elites" create invisible but omnipresent enemies requiring vigilant opposition.

- **Victimhood narratives**: Groups define themselves through historical oppression by enemies. Serbian narratives of Ottoman oppression, Shi'a narratives of Sunni persecution, white nationalist narratives of "white genocide" create contemporary enemies from historical conflicts.

- **Civilizational discourse**: "Clash of civilizations" narratives (Huntington) that structure global politics as conflict between essential cultural blocs—West vs. Islam, Christianity vs. secularism, democracy vs. authoritarianism.

**5. Affective Mechanisms: The Emotions of Enmity**

The friend-enemy distinction operates through emotional dynamics:

- **Fear and threat perception**: Psychological research shows humans are predisposed to threat detection and negativity bias. Politicians exploit this by amplifying threats from enemies—"They're coming to take your jobs/freedom/way of life." *(Political psychology research)*

- **Hatred and disgust**: Visceral emotional responses to enemies that bypass rational deliberation. Disgust particularly powerful because it treats enemies as contaminants requiring elimination. *(Nussbaum, "From Disgust to Humanity")*

- **Righteous indignation**: Moral outrage at enemy transgressions justifies aggressive response. "How dare they!" becomes basis for mobilization against enemies.

- **Pride and superiority**: In-group pride constituted through comparison with inferior out-groups. "We" are civilized/pure/enlightened; "they" are barbaric/corrupt/benighted.

- **Ressentiment**: Nietzsche's concept of festering resentment toward those perceived as oppressors. Slave morality transforms inability to defeat enemies into moral superiority over them.

**6. Escalation Dynamics**

Once established, friend-enemy distinctions tend to intensify through:

- **Security dilemmas**: Actions taken for defense appear as aggression to the other side, provoking counter-measures in endless spiral. Each side's preparation for war with the enemy makes war more likely. *(International relations theory)*

- **Martyrdom and retaliation cycles**: Each side's martyrs demand vengeance, creating cycles of violence. Israeli-Palestinian conflict, Sunni-Shia violence, ethnic conflicts in Balkans all show this pattern.

- **Outbidding dynamics**: Political entrepreneurs compete to prove loyalty by being more extreme toward enemies. Who can be toughest on terrorism/crime/immigration? This ratchets up enmity as moderates get outflanked.

- **Dehumanization spirals**: As conflict intensifies, each atrocity justifies worse treatment of enemies, further removing moral constraints. "They attacked us, so all limits are off."

---

### Typology of Enmity: Varieties of the Friend-Enemy Distinction

Not all enmities are alike. Schmitt's binary tends to flatten distinctions between fundamentally different types of friend-enemy relationships. A comprehensive analysis requires distinguishing:

**1. Existential vs. Contingent Enmity**

- **Existential enmity** (Schmitt's focus): Enemy threatens one's very existence or form of life. "It's them or us." No possibility of coexistence. Examples: Nazi Germany's view of Jews (racial threat to Aryan existence), Islamist view of crusader-Zionist conspiracy, Cold War fear of communist world domination.

- **Contingent enmity**: Opposition based on specific conflicts of interest that could potentially be resolved. Trade disputes, territorial conflicts, policy disagreements. Enemy today might be friend tomorrow if circumstances change.

The crucial question: When does contingent enmity escalate to existential enmity? How do normal political conflicts become life-or-death struggles?

**2. Absolute vs. Real vs. Conventional Enemies**

Schmitt himself distinguishes (in Theory of the Partisan):

- **Absolute enemy**: Must be destroyed completely. No recognition, no rules, total war. The enemy is evil incarnate. Examples: Nazi view of Jews, Islamist view of apostates, genocide perpetrators' view of victims.

- **Real enemy**: Genuine adversary in conflict over concrete issues. Can respect enemy as worthy opponent. European interstate warfare under jus publicum Europaeum—recognize each other as legitimate sovereigns even while fighting.

- **Conventional enemy**: Ritually opposed but within established framework. Sports rivalry, electoral competition, agonal friendship in Nietzsche's sense. Opponents within shared game rather than existential threats.

Schmitt argues liberal universalism transforms real enemies into absolute enemies by moralizing conflict—enemy becomes "aggressor," "war criminal," "evil empire" who must be utterly vanquished rather than accommodated.

**3. External vs. Internal Enemies**

- **External enemies**: Beyond borders, threatening from outside. Traditional warfare against foreign powers. Clear spatial separation allows for "us" vs. "them."

- **Internal enemies**: Within the political community but identified as traitors, fifth column, hostile elements. More dangerous because their hidden presence creates paranoia. Requires vigilance to identify and purge.

- **The enemy within**: Particularly destabilizing because blurs friend-enemy line within community. Who can be trusted? Witch hunts, spy scares, red scares, McCarthyism all reflect paranoia about internal enemies.

Schmitt's theory of democracy requires homogeneity precisely because internal enemies threaten unity. Liberal pluralism allows internal enemies to undermine political will.

**4. Personal vs. Structural Enmity**

- **Personal enmity**: Between individuals based on their specific actions and characteristics. Traditional blood feuds, personal rivalries, individual conflicts.

- **Structural enmity**: Built into social positions regardless of individual characteristics. Bourgeoisie vs. proletariat (Marx), colonizer vs. colonized (Fanon), men vs. women (radical feminism). One's position in structure creates enmity independent of personal feelings.

This distinction matters for possibility of resolution: Personal enmities might be overcome through reconciliation; structural enmities require systemic transformation.

**5. Symmetrical vs. Asymmetrical Enmity**

- **Symmetrical enmity**: Roughly equal powers in mutual opposition. Classical warfare between sovereign states. Each recognizes other as legitimate enemy with capacity to kill.

- **Asymmetrical enmity**: Vast power imbalance between enemies. Imperial power vs. colonized resistance, state vs. terrorists, hegemon vs. rogue state. Weak side often adopts asymmetric methods (guerrilla war, terrorism) because conventional conflict is impossible.

Schmitt's Theory of the Partisan examines how asymmetric conflict transforms nature of enmity—partisan doesn't wear uniform, blends with population, uses irregular methods. This provokes harsher response from conventional forces.

**6. Legalized vs. Criminalized Enmity**

- **Legalized enmity**: Enemy recognized as legitimate belligerent with rights under laws of war. POW status, Geneva Conventions, diplomatic immunity. Can be enemy without being criminal.

- **Criminalized enmity**: Enemy defined as lawbreaker without legitimate grievances. "Terrorist," "pirate," "war criminal" denies political status. Schmitt argues this makes war more total because strips enemy of protections.

The shift from legalized to criminalized enmity marks transition from classical to humanitarian international law—but Schmitt argues this humanitarian turn actually intensifies violence.

**7. Identity-Based vs. Interest-Based Enmity**

- **Identity-based**: Enmity rooted in what groups ARE—ethnicity, religion, race, civilization. Enemy is enemy by nature of their identity. More difficult to overcome because identity is less malleable than interests.

- **Interest-based**: Enmity rooted in conflicting goals or claims—territory, resources, power. More amenable to negotiation and compromise because interests can be traded.

Realist international relations theory assumes interest-based enmity (states pursue power/security). Constructivist theory emphasizes how interests are shaped by identities and ideas. *(Wendt, "Anarchy Is What States Make of It")*

**8. Chosen vs. Ascribed Enmity**

- **Chosen enmity**: Based on actions, beliefs, choices. "We oppose them because of what they DO." Can potentially change through conversion, reform, apology.

- **Ascribed enmity**: Based on innate, unchangeable characteristics. "We oppose them because of what they ARE." No possibility of redemption—enemy status is permanent.

Racial enemies (ascribed) versus ideological enemies (chosen) have different dynamics. Can a communist become ex-communist? Can a Jew become ex-Jew? Nazi racial antisemitism made enmity ascribed and permanent.

---

### The Political Psychology of Enmity: Cognitive and Social Mechanisms

Modern social psychology and cognitive science reveal deep mechanisms that make friend-enemy distinctions psychologically compelling:

**1. Minimal Group Paradigm**

Henri Tajfel's experiments demonstrate that even arbitrary group assignments (blue vs. red, Kandinsky vs. Klee) immediately create:
- In-group favoritism and out-group discrimination
- Beliefs that in-group is more heterogeneous, out-group more homogeneous
- Readiness to allocate resources preferentially to in-group

This suggests friend-enemy dynamics may be deeply embedded in human social cognition—we automatically categorize into "us" and "them" with minimal prompting. *(Tajfel et al., "Social Categorization and Intergroup Behaviour," 1971)*

**2. Terror Management Theory**

Humans' awareness of mortality creates existential anxiety. Cultural worldviews and group identities provide symbolic immortality. Reminders of death strengthen:
- Allegiance to in-group values and authorities
- Hostility toward out-groups who threaten worldview
- Support for violence against enemies

Post-9/11 research showed mortality salience increased support for aggressive foreign policy, punitive domestic policy, and charismatic leaders. Terror makes us seek strong leaders who will protect us from enemies. *(Greenberg et al., "Terror Management Theory")*

**3. Moral Foundations Theory**

Jonathan Haidt's research identifies multiple moral foundations:
- Care/harm
- Fairness/cheating
- Loyalty/betrayal
- Authority/subversion
- Sanctity/degradation
- Liberty/oppression
